#### [Setup row level security](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=6)** So [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] security can be pretty granular and without too much effort, I can actually block people from seeing certain columns in tables. I can also block people from seeing rows in tables. So for columns, it's actually very, very simple. I can go into a particular database and into a particular table and say this Sales, this special deals table. And if I look at this table and look at permissions, I can add people. So for example, my HRAdmin account. I can add him to this table and I can give him permissions to do certain things on the table, whether it's alter or select or whatever. But one of the things you'll notice when I click down in this permission, so let's say I give him select permission, I get this button that says column permissions, so I can click on that and then I can go through and say, "You know what? He only gets access to these five columns or whatever it is" and hit "Okay." And if I do that and run a SELECT statement with my HR user, he'll see this table, but he'll only see these five columns. So that's semi useful depending on if I'm trying to hide information from my users. I can also do that by creating views to limit what they can see. But what if I want to limit them to what rows they can see? And that's what this is all about. And here you don't see a row level permission. This is done in a slightly different way.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=99)** It's not necessarily a permission set. So let's click "Cancel" out of this box, and I have this demo. Now, there's a decent amount of code here, but we're going to kind of walk through each piece. So the first thing I'm going to do is create three users in my database, a Manager, Sales1, and Sales2. So Salesperson1, Salesperson2. You'll notice I'm creating this without logins because I don't care if they can log on to the server or not. So we're going to do this inside of our HR database. So we'll just change our context here to HR. Okay. And we'll run that piece. So that basically created three users. So if I go to security, expand users, and refresh this, you'll see I now have Manager, Sales1, and Sales2. Okay. I want to create a tiny table in here. So I'm going to create a table called Sales. It's going to hold an OrderID, the Rep who sold it, a Product, and account. So for that I'll create the table. There we go. And we're going to insert some Orders. And I didn't put OrderID, so let me just add that real quick. I'm going to add Order 2, 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5. So just real simple Order numbers. This obviously would not be part of a production schema, but not a problem. So now I'm going to run this insert statement to put these things into Sales, and you'll see these first three Sales were done by Salesperson1 that is the SalesRep. Here's the product they sold, here's the quantity. The second three were done by Salesperson Number 2. Okay. So we'll just run that insert statement. Done.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=194)** Now, if I SELECT all from that table, you should essentially see that that table contains the data we just inserted. No big surprise there, but right now I can SELECT FROM it. These users cannot. So I'm going to skip down to the bottom here for a second. Ignore all that. Down here I have some code that uses a special EXECUTE AS statement. So I can essentially run this SELECT statement as though I were logged in with this user and I as an admin have the right to do that. So you'll see, I'll just do the first one here. I'll highlight this first guy. If I attempt to EXECUTE AS Sales1, SELECT all from sales, you'll notice I get SELECT permission denied. Okay. So I want you to see that the SELECT is happening as that user and not as me. So if I scroll back up right here, I'm going to grant select on those tables. So essentially the statement for that GRANT SELECT ON table name TO user. So I'm going to GRANT SELECT ON SALES TO Manager, TO Sales1, and TO Sales2. Okay. And I'll hit "Execute." So now all three of those users have select to this table. So if I come back down here and I'm going to highlight all three of these users and all three of their SELECT statements and hit "Go," you'll notice I get three queries returned, and all the queries have all the data. Okay. I don't necessarily want that. What I want is when Sales user Number 1 looks at the table, they can see their sales and when Sales2 looks at the table, they can see their sales and when Manager looks, he can see everything. That's how it would like to set up. So in order to do that, we have to set up some row level security.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=289)** So the first thing I'm going to do to make this work is I'm going to create a brand new schema just called SECURITY. I'll execute that. And you'll see that now in my HR database, if I refresh here, I have this brand new SECURITY schema. And I'm going to use this schema to store the functions I need in order to make this row level security work. So this first function I'm going to create is a security predicate. And essentially what this is going to do is it's going to take a SalesRep name, so it's going to be passed in as part of the function, and it's going to return a table and it has schema binding on. And this is all kind of some function creation stuff that you have to worry about too much right now. But what I'm going to do is I'm going to return the value of one as a column name called function_securitypredicate_result. And essentially, I'm going to compare the SalesRep value you passed in with the username. So username is a function that basically returns the current user. So if I were to run select USER_NAME and I can show you that real quick. I'll just come up here and put another column in, select USER_NAME and run this, you'll see it kicks back dbo because in this database I'm dbo. Okay. So that's what it comes back as. I'm database owner. So basically what this function is doing, it says compare the SalesRep value you gave me to USER_NAME. And if -- so say, if you gave me SalesRep1 and I'm SalesRep1,
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=384)** then say "Yes. I'm able to do this." Return a one. Just to be a one in a column in a row, or if the current USER_NAME is Manager, return a one. So the one essentially says you have the rights to look at this data or you don't. Okay. And so if the username matches the running username, we're good to go. And if you're a Manager, you're good to go. Okay. So that's just the function. That's all that's doing is comparing the current user to the user we're selecting in. So now in order to apply this function and use it to do row level security, what we need is a security policy. Okay. So let me create this real quick, go, and now we have our function and then we're going to come down to this portion here. We're going to create a security policy. So we're going to create a policy called SalesFilter, and we're going to add a filter predicate. So the filter predicate is what's letting us do row level security filtering data. Okay. We're going to use this function and pass in SalesRep, and SalesRep is the column name. So remember, if I go back up to this column, it has a SalesRep column. So we're passing in the SalesRep column to this function. So the SalesRep column contains the name of the user, remember, either Sales1 or Sales2, and then it compares that value from that column to the actual running user.
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=479)** So essentially that's what we've done here and we put it on the Sales table. So now when we select, it's going to pass in a value from the column for all the rows for the SalesRep and compare that with my currently logged on user. Okay. So I'll just go ahead and create this security policy. I'll execute it. And now that is in place on that table. So now what happens, when I go ahead and run as Sales1, as I'm selecting this, it takes the value from the SalesRep column, which remembers either Sales1 or Sales2. And here, let me just run that select real quick so we can look at it as we talk about it. So here's -- oh, and see, I'm not either of these people, so now I'm blocked. So because of this filter, I'm not Manager and I'm not Sales1, so I'm actually blocked out. So what I'm going to do real quick is I'm going to run this as Manager just so we can see the data. So you'll see when I run as Manager, I get the entire table. And remember, this column was Sales1 or Sales2, the name of the person who actually sold the product. And so with this predicate, it passes the value from SalesRep in and if that ends up matching my user, so if I'm Sales1, then return me all the values from all the rows that have Sales1 in this column. Or if I'm Manager, which is how this happened, is how I got all six, and if I'm Sales2 return me just the Sales2 rows, and if anybody else, sorry, you can't see it. Which is why I was unable to see it when I ran the select * up above.
>
> **[9:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=571)** So I'll execute here as Sales1. And you'll see he only gets three rows and it's the rows that belong to Salesperson1. And Salesperson2, same thing. He gets three rows, but only the things that belong to him, and Manager can see it all. So I'll just run all three of these just so you can see them side by side. SalesRep1 gets 1, 2 gets 2, and Manager sees it all. So it's a little complicated and it's a little harder than just setting up security by using the GUI, but between a function and this function can do anything you want, right? It's going to essentially take in a column name, and then you will then base the value of whatever it's passed in. So in this case, whatever values are in that column, you can create a function here that does whatever you want. So based on the value in the column, do I want to compare this to the currently logged on user? Do I want to look at dates and times to see if this data is usable? Do I want, I mean, this guy is kind of the limit, but if you're going to do it for security purposes, you're typically comparing it to the currently running user in order to filter this data out. Okay. So that's how you can do row level security inside SQL Server to make sure that even when people have access to the same table, they're only seeing the things they need to see. Now, alternatively, you could do this with a function that returns a table, possibly passing in the username. You could create views, but with views you would have to create a view for User1 and a view for User2.
>
> **[11:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/setup-row-level-security?u=76281980&t=666)** But with this, I can just still use the same table and I can filter that data out. And the beautiful thing here is because this is filtered row level at the table, if I were to create a view based on this table, this still applies. So the user coming through the view would still be row limited. So it's a good way to hide sensitive data when there's multiple users that access the same table and they only need to see subsets of that particular data.

#### [Understand data masking](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-data-masking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-data-masking?u=76281980&t=6)** So among all the ways you can protect data in your [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]], one of them is data masking. So I can block access to rows, I can block access to columns, I can encrypt data, but what if I need to see some portion of data but not necessarily reveal all of said data? And that's what data masking is all about. And it'll make more sense here when we walk through this demo. So in the WideWorldImporters database, I'm just going to make a new query, there is a column in the people table that contains email. So if I say select all from Application.People and I hit run, you'll notice over here that there's a login name that contains the email of that particular person. And while that's not necessarily super sensitive info, you can mask email addresses so that someone could see a little bit about it, but not see the whole thing. So it's sort of like when you call the bank and they say, "Oh, is this the credit card ending in 1, 2, 3, 4," they can see the last four of your credit card. They can't see the whole thing. Same thing with give me the last four of your social, so you can mask that data. So I have it, but it's not available for everybody. So to do that, we have to apply a data mask to this table. >
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-data-masking?u=76281980&t=103)** And this is a great article to go look at if you want to understand all the different things that you can data mask. And you can audit all this kind of stuff to really keep tabs of what's happening on your server. And then if you do fall victim to either someone trying to hack your data or some sort of a malicious attack, even by an internal person, hopefully these logs and this audit can help you figure out exactly when on and possibly even help you recover your information.

### Lesson 7: Understanding and Designing SQL Server Tables

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Module 4 introduction](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/module-4-introduction?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/module-4-introduction?u=76281980&t=6)** Knowing how to design, tune, and optimize [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]], really level up your skills as a Database Admin. Let's look at DB design, including table design, views, and change tracking. Then look at [[Performance Tuning]] and optimization, including indexes and [[Statistics]] as well as isolation levels and execution plans.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Microsoft SQL Server|Sql server]] (1), [[Performance Tuning]] (1), [[Statistics]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** sql (1)

#### [Learning objectives](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/learning-objectives?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/learning-objectives?u=76281980&t=6)** Welcome to our lesson on understanding and designing [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] tables. In order to properly manage a database, it helps to have an understanding of at least basic [[Database Design]]. This lesson will provide a foundation for database design and aims to give you an understanding of various table types and structures, as well as a glimpse into tracking changes in tables. This lesson covers a review of table design, understanding special table types, creating views, using change [[Data Tracking]] and change data capture. Let's get started.

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-special-table-types?u=76281980&t=6)** So in general, when you're working with [[SQL]] [[Databases]], you're mostly going to use regular old tables. Now, there are a couple special table types that I want to point out. We're not going to demo each one of these, but I want you to at least understand what they are. So as you're going through and working with SQL if you encounter them, you know what they are and you know how you could potentially use them for your systems. So as it happens, the WideWorldImporters database has some of these tables already set up in them. And you'll see here the standard table has this table icon that just sort of looks like grids and rows and columns. Okay. That's a standard table. You also have system version tables which have this little clock on it right there. And there are some of those, obviously, right here in WideWorldImporters. And essentially what a system version table is. You'll notice if I expand this, I have Application.People is the name of my table. But I also have Application.People_Archive. And if you look in there, it has the same columns as the columns that are there for Application.People. And what that is, is it gives you a history. So as things have been changing in the system, you have this archive. So if I select all from Application.People, and run it. And then I also select from Application.People_Archive,
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-special-table-types?u=76281980&t=102)** and run that. You'll see I still get data, but I only get, in this case, 961 rows of it. As it here I'll select them both. So I get 961 rows in this archive, and I get. 1111 rows down in this full table. And you'll notice here, for example, if I look at 14 in my real table, it's Lily code, which corresponds to what I'm seeing up here. But what's up here in this archive is an older version of this same record. So something changed, something got modified, and this system version table, which also called the temporal table, is storing some of the histories and storing some of the old data. So it's a way for you to go back and see how things existed prior to the change in the main table. Whereas normally, data is gone or data's changed and you can't see it anymore. So this kind of gives you a little bit of a history and a little bit of an archive you can go back to. And you'll notice here that 14 there's Lily code again and I can actually narrow it down. So I'll say where PersonID = 14. We'll just pick on this Lily code person, and you'll see she's in the archive 17 different times. So these are 17 different versions of Lily code. And you can see some of these are like other language changed, whereas it didn't here, and it tells you some valid from
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-special-table-types?u=76281980&t=198)** data over here. And if you look at regular people here, again, let me do this so we can just see her standard real record as well side by side, so you'll see as I come over here, some of these valid to's and from's changed, some of these dates changed on her. And that looks like that might have been what changed, a lot of the other day it looks the same, but I have 14 different rows, so 14 different histories of Lily code, and that's all stored inside these system version tables. Now, to make one of those, if you right click "Table," you can say new and then instead of table, which is what we normally do, you say temporal table and the only version of temporal table that exists is this system version table. And that lets you set up these kind of tables that can store history. You also have some very specific types like ledgers and graphs that I'm not going to really dig into, but those are things that lets you set up some different kinds of data. Graph tables essentially give you a way to model many to many relationships in [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]]. So a node is like a table, and an edge is like a relationship and it gives you a many to many model. You can do that with regular tables. You can create two tables with a many to many join table in the middle. This just kind of gives you a different way to do that. You also have memory optimized tables, which are great for lookup tables. Portions of these are stored in memory and they're only hardened to disk every so often, so they're much faster. So they're great for lookup tables data that you have to reference a
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-special-table-types?u=76281980&t=292)** ton because you're going to get it from memory and not from hard drive. So it's a little bit quicker. You also have external tables. This is for storing data in external sources such as [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]] and then file tables. So file tables are kind of neat. I'm not going to do a full blown demo. I believe there are some in this WideWorldImporters database if you want to dig into them, but essentially with a file table, I can create what looks like a table in SQL. So it would look like this kind of a table like people or people archive, but it could be called invoice files, for example. And when I read from that, I can actually read a file out of that table and send it down to a client, like read the bytes out of the file and they now have that file that they can access. But what's really happening is that file table saving to a directory on the hard drive on SQL Server and I'm able to use file streaming to move things back and forth. So if I write a file to SQL, it ends up as a copy in this folder on the hard drive, and I can go the other way so I can copy files to a folder on the hard drive, and then when I query the table I can actually see those files. So it gives you a connection to be able to store files, "store files in SQL Server" without actually storing anything in SQL Server. Because you can, and we saw in some other lessons in this table particular here, photo is a column that's available in this Application.People table. So photo is a file, right? It's a type of file. And if I look at Application.People, and I look at columns,
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-special-table-types?u=76281980&t=388)** you'll see photo is a binary max. So I'm essentially streaming binary data into a column and storing it as a photo. Alternatively, I can do these file stream tables or these file tables where I'm actually storing a photo on the hard drive, and then I can just relate from my Application.People table to that photo rather than streaming it in and out of binary data in a column. So it's just another option for you. So all those are accessible if you right click "Table" and say new and, again, I'm not going to demo all of these. So I just wanted to make you aware of their existence so that you can use that information going forward as you design your database.

#### [Create views](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=6)** So if you've gone through any of the other lessons in this series, you'll see that we've often referenced tables and we've pulled data in and out of tables, and they are truly the heart of a database. That is where everything lives. But the other piece of using a database is you're constantly trying to read data in and out of said database, and you want it in a form that is friendly to your users and friendly to your applications. And how it's stored in the tables isn't always designed to be user friendly. It's more designed to be appropriate for either relational database or for speed if you're setting up a reporting server or whatever the case may be, and often your data is scattered all over multiple tables because that makes sense from a design standpoint, but not necessarily from an access standpoint. So I'm going to show you what a view is and how you can use it to start creating literally just that views of your data. So I'm just going to start a new query in the WideWorldImporters database, and I'm going to select some data from some tables. And this is a fairly common thing that you would have to do. So let's just select all from Orders and it's actually Sales schema.Orders. So we'll do that. >
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=99)** So we'll select OrderID, but now I immediately need customer data which is in another table. >
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=142)** Okay. >
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=235)** customer or I could look at date ranges. I could do all kinds of stuff with it. But here's the thing. I don't want to have to type all of this every time I want this set of data. This is three different tables, technically two, but I'm using Application.People twice, so it's three different joins and I'm pulling five or six different columns. I just want this to exist so that I can query it later. So that's where views come in. Views in [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] are stored sets of [[Transact-SQL (T-SQL)|Transact-SQL]] that return data. Okay. So to create a view, I can come up here and I can add the line, CREATE VIEW and then give it a name. So I would call this OrderSalesCustomerList, something like that. And then I just have to say AS, that's just the syntax. CREATE VIEW, name, AS, and then specify my SELECT statement. Now when I run this, instead of running the SELECT statement like it did before, it's going to create a view that stores the SELECT statement into it. So I'll hit "F5." There you go. And now this view exists. So if I look under views, you'll see I have this OrderSalesCustomerList. That is now under my Views folder, and this allows me to just run select * from OrderSalesCstomerList.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=331)** That's it. I only have to know that. And when I run it, I get the exact same data that I got from this big hairy query up here. Okay. So a view lets me encapsulate, select data, and just rerun it over and over. Now, the nice thing is, as far as the query engine is concerned and as far as the way you write T-[[SQL]] code, this SELECT statement still returning me data. This SELECT statement could still be a table. So nothing stops me from saying "What if I have this OrderID and I also want to see the order lines?" Well, right from this view I can join Sales.OrderLines on, and I would need this whole view name if I want to type it in, which is fine. And I can say on this OrderID equals, and I'll just shortcut this because, again, I don't like typing, ol for OrderLine .OrderID. And now, I've joined OrderLines directly to this Sales.Order view and then I can run that. And now you'll see I get my my first five columns. That's what comes from OrderSalesCstomerList, right, through this email address. And then right here, because I have star, I get all the data in my OrderLines table. So I could further narrow that down and say I only want to see the stock item and the description and the quantity, just like I did with my other SELECT, but I can join right to that view. So views return data that look like tables,
>
> **[7:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=427)** but the data is coming from somewhere, it's coming from underlying tables and then that data is bubbling up being saved, and now it's basically a stored query that just runs every time I call that view. Now, there's a few pieces and parts that are a little more complicated with views as far as security and how they access underlying tables. And I'm not going to get into too much of that. But what's interesting with views and tables is as long as you have what's called ownership chaining on, and in this database, it should be. So I'll just show you this real quick. WideWorldImporters, properties, options. So you'll have cross database ownership chaining and then you'll have cross database ownership chaining is false. So I can't go cross database with this because it's not enabled, but I can internally. And what ownership chaining means is if this view and these tables all exist and have the same owners, and in this case they don't because this is dbo and these tables are in their own schemas, one's in sales, one's in application, if they all have the same owners, all I need access to is the view and I can still query the data. I won't be able to access the tables directly because I don't have access to them, but because I have access to the view and they have the same owner, I can still see the database, the tables, the data in them. Now, if they have different owners, as is the case here,
>
> **[8:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=522)** access to this view -- read access to the view will not be enough. I'll also need access to these tables. I don't want to get bogged down in that in this lesson, I just wanted to bring it up because as you start creating views and as you start storing queries in them, you might run into that. If you start giving people access to your views and you don't have chaining set up in a way that they can access the tables. So it's called ownership chaining, and it's a concept you might have to dig up as you start creating views. Again, a little out of the scope of this lesson, but I just did want to mention that while we were here. So that's pretty much it. Views are just stored select statements. You don't have to create them with a script. In fact, there's an okay GUI that exists. If you right click and say new view, you actually get kind of a graphical interface. So I could do the same thing I just did with script in here by going down, getting my Orders table, so I selected that one, and selecting my people table. Those two are now selected, orders and people, and then I can hit "Add," and then you can see it actually starts graphically to show you every place that these have joined. So this PersonID actually points to the SalesPerson, the PickedByPerson, the ContactPerson, the CustomerID, and something else done here, LastEditedBy.
>

And really all we were focusing on in this particular lesson is that views allow you to store select statements and then you can later utilize those views as if they were tables to retrieve data to
>
> **[11:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-views?u=76281980&t=710)** join to other tables. You can even insert into them. There's a couple restrictions around that and you could update data and views and all of that then trickles down to the underlying tables, and it just gives you a great shortcut rather than having to type out these long, complicated queries every time.

#### [Understand change data capture](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-change-data-capture?u=76281980)

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-change-data-capture?u=76281980&t=6)** So change data capture is a feature in [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] that allows you to capture when data changes in your database. So anytime DML statements are run that cause changes, you can track and capture that information to see what's changing and how and possibly use that for recovery. ### Lesson 8: Performance Tuning and Optimizing SQL

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Learning objectives](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/learning-objectives-22311171?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/learning-objectives-22311171?u=76281980&t=6)** Welcome to our lesson on [[Performance Tuning]] and optimizing [[SQL]]. Keeping [[Databases]] online and backed up is only half the battle. To be useful, databases have to be able to provide data to users and applications in a timely fashion. This is where performance tuning plays a huge role. This lesson will provide a look at the performance tools available in [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]]. We cover understanding isolation levels, using execution plans, configuring clustered indexes as well as non-clustered indexes, and using index [[Statistics]]. Let's get started.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Performance Tuning]] (2), [[Databases]] (2), [[SQL]] (1), [[Microsoft SQL Server|Sql server]] (1), [[Statistics]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** sql (2)

#### [Understand isolation levels](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-isolation-levels?u=76281980)

#### [Use execution plans](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=6)** So behind [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] is a thing called the query optimizer. And essentially what this is doing is every time you run a query, it's trying to figure out the best way to access the data in the database. And it's making use of things like indexes and [[Statistics]], and it's making all these choices to try to figure out a way to execute your query efficiently. You can see the plan that it's putting together using a thing called execution plans, and that information can help you to tune your database and or your queries to get the best performance out of them. So that's what we're going to take a look at here. So I'm just going to fire up a new query in my WideWorldImporters database here. And I'm just going to do a simple select statement and I will select all from Sales.OrdersCopy. Now, I'm using this table because it's raw data with no indexes or anything else on it. And before I run this, I want to do a couple of things. One, I want to go over to the query menu and I want to check include actual execution plan. And the other thing you can do here is you can say display estimated execution plan. And what that will do is that just showed me an estimated execution plan. So this is what it thinks this query is going to do when I run it. I haven't actually run it yet. So this is purely an estimation, in this case, a pretty good one, but it thinks it's going to select data and most of the work's going to go into a table scan. That's fairly accurate.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=99)** You'll notice that there's some data here that's all zeros and it can't fully calculate everything because this isn't the actual plan. But if I execute the query, after a second, you'll see this execution plan tab popped back up. And this one should look pretty similar to that estimation you just saw. But what this is telling me is what the query optimizer had to do and how the data had to be retrieved for this query. So this can help you find things in your query that aren't running efficiently or that need to be tuned, or maybe things in your database where you're missing indexes or something of that nature. A good rule of thumb when you're looking at execution plans, scans are bad. So scans basically mean that it had to go through this whole table to find what I needed. And when you select all from a particular table, that's not all that surprising because I said give me everything from the table. But what if -- and just let's take a look at the results, we can pick some dates here, what if I wanted delivery dates? So I had a where clause where delivery -- expected delivery date is greater than, we'll go with 5-9-2013, and expected delivery date is less than,
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=180)** we'll say 5-9-2014. So there are 73,591 rows in this table. I'm going to look for a range of delivery dates over this year. Okay. I'm just going to go ahead and run this query and it's going to run and it's going to give me my execution plan. And you'll see the results, I got 19,000 rows, so I definitely got fewer rows, about a third of them of the whole table fall within these date ranges. And if you look at the execution plan, it still did a table scan, but you'll see 100% of the cost of this query went into scanning this table. So what that means is to find these 19,000 rows. It actually had to look at all almost 80,000 rows. It went through absolutely everything to find those rows. It gets even worse if I say, select all where the OrderID equals,
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=241)** I don't know, 1234, 1, 2, 3, 4. That's one row. Now, this is a relatively small table, so that still returned relatively quickly. But if you look at the execution plan, again, it table scanned the entire table. So imagine I had a list of orders written down on a piece of paper and they weren't in any sort of numerical order. And I said, "Hey, go find Order 1234" and I handed you 70,000 sheets of paper and you have to go through all of them. And here's the kicker. You don't know that there aren't two Order 1234. So it's not like you can stop as soon as you find the first one. You have to go through all 70,000 to find the orders 1234. That would be inefficient and slow. And that's the same thing happening in [[SQL]]. This is inefficient and this is slow. So anytime you see a table scan, index scan, anything like that in here, it's woefully slow and it's something that you want to maybe look at fixing. Now, conversely, if I just select all from Sales.Order, not Copy for both of these queries, each of these tables has these versions, have the indexes. So if I run this, here's my execution plan. I still got my 19,000 rows under this other table, but you'll see here it actually did a clustered index scan, which isn't much better than a table scan, but instead it scan the
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=335)** clustered index. The clustered index is, and there's a whole lesson on clustered indexes and what they were -- what they are and how they work, but essentially it is an index that is -- defines the order in which your data in your table is stored. So your data is stored in this order. Which means if I'm looking for something that's part of that index, I can find it without having to look at the whole table. I believe on this table that the clustered index is on OrderID, so we should see a bit of a difference when I run this query. So I'll hit "Execute," "Execution plan," and so you'll see this time this did a clustered index seek. So rather than scanning, you'll see the number of rows read, it can't point to it. But that right in the middle of that yellow maybe six things down, number of rows read is one, meaning it used the clustered index and think of a clustered index kind of like a dictionary. So if I had to do a dictionary and said, go find this entry, you can pretty much skip right to that entry because you know how the alphabet works. You can jump right to that entry. So that's what this did. Because there's a clustered index on OrderID, it jumped right to it. So no scan, no nothing. Read one row. Okay. Much more efficient. Whereas with this one, you'll see it did the scan and if I hover over it, it read all 73,595 rows and the actual number of rows was 19,887 that it returned. So these query plans can sort of help see -- help you see how your
>
> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=431)** code is running. Now, if you use something a little more complex. So in WideWorldImporters I have a view here called OrderSalesCustomerList. And this actually gets data from the Application.Person table as well as the Sales.Order table. And I'll just select all from this little view and I'll hit "Execute." And this does more than just select from one table. It's selecting specific columns and it's doing some joining. And so this execution plan gets more complex and these execution plans can get very, very complex, very fast. And you'll see with this one, you start to see these percentages and where the work was done. So here you see I did a hash match. This is an inner join. So essentially this is how it joined one table to the other. And here I'm going to script this view as create to my clipboard so I can just pull out the select. So this is the select that I'm running inside that view. So I'm selecting some columns and I'm doing inner joins from Sales.Order to Application.People. So these hash joins are basically these inner joins happening where I have to say, well go find the CustomerID in my order table and match it to the CustomerID in my people table. And I do that twice. So you'll see the hash join here. Then it scans this index, which is a non clustered index scan. And this is in, you can see down at the bottom where it says object. This is the Application.People FullName.
>
> **[8:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=525)** Well that's scanning. And then down here I do another hash join because I joined this table two times and then I do another index scan to get the PersonID, the full name, and the email address from the table for my customer. And then down here, I finally do a clustered index scan to pull out my primary sales, my OrderID. Okay. So these are all the scans I'm doing. So again, scans aren't the greatest in the world, so we don't have necessarily a great set of indexes for this particular query to work with. So there's certainly some tuning we can do. Now, we're going to continue to look at execution plans when we do lessons on clustered indexing and on non clustered indexing. And as I said, these execution plans can get very, very complicated, very, very fast. And I don't want to go into too much more detail in this particular lesson. What I wanted to show you in this lesson is how you can cause these execution plans to run and how you can look and see where the hotspots are. So you look at the cost value. That's 39%, this one is 18%, and this should all add up to 100. So I can find that what I was doing most of the time was these two hash joins followed by this index scan here at the end, which was 42% of my total cost. So I can use these execution plans to see all the different things that are happening, and the goal is to find things that are bad. Again, scans are bad. Look for scans, and you want to try to encourage things like parallelism and things like that where you're actually using
>
> **[10:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-execution-plans?u=76281980&t=621)** multiple threads to go [[Fetch]] data. So for this lesson, go run some queries. You can kind of mess around with it, play with some, look at the execution plans, and it kind of helps give you a sense of where you might have some [[Performance Tuning]] opportunities in your server.

And indexes are very, very important in doing just that, indexing into tables to find things. And there are two primary kinds of indexes that SQL Server utilizes. In this lesson, we're going to focus on clustered indexes. Think of a clustered index as a dictionary. All of the data in a dictionary is stored from A to Z, and if I need to find a particular [[Microsoft Word|word]], panda, I flip to p, pa, pan, panda. #### [Use index statistics](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-index-statistics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-index-statistics?u=76281980&t=6)** So in this lesson, we want to take a look at index [[Statistics]]. Now indexes, clustered and non-clustered indexes, are used to help improve the performance of your queries and your [[SQL]] Servers. With statistics that's information that the index tracks about your tables to help it make decisions about which indexes to use and how to go about getting your data. Now, in general, these are updated automatically and you don't have to worry about them too much, but sometimes they get out of whack and you need to know what they are, how to look at them, and how to rebuild them if need be. So let's take a look at just that. So here I've got just some sample code and we're going to walk through what all this does. So before we get started, I want to turn off updating statistics for this particular index so that we can kind of mess around with the statistics on our own. So to do that, I'm going to expand the database. That's why it's true. I'm just going to go ahead and turn this to false. So now my statistics will not be auto recomputed and we can see what happens when they get kind of out of date. >
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-index-statistics?u=76281980&t=102)** clustered index scan and I'm using the order -- the primary key, in order to do that scan. If I go back and look at my statistics, and again, this is the information it's using when it's trying to figure out how to query and I hit "F5," you'll see it thinks there's still 73,591 rows. So that's the information it's going to use to attempt to make its decision on what to do with my query and how to optimize it. So if I come back up here and I run this and I hit execute and I look at the execution plan, now I'm doing a table scan because now I don't have the primary key anymore. And it -- that's fine. It didn't make -- it didn't have much of a decision to make because the primary key is gone, but it needed the statistics to make this decision. And obviously, with this kind of table and this kind of data set, there wasn't much of a decision to make. But as things get larger and as you have bigger result sets, you can see how having this information is going to be important to the query optimizer. So if I had auto update statistics turned on, periodically, it updates those things and it typically takes care of it. But occasionally, and I've seen this in service with very large [[Databases]], we'll run update statistics throughout the day or we'll have jobs that are standing by that we can run it because you'll just get some weird behavior and it's just not updating statistics enough
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-index-statistics?u=76281980&t=383)** or it's not updating them -- yeah, it's just not updating them fast enough. And so your query starts to slow down. So we would just run update stats occasionally to help fix some performance problems. So to that end, let's do that now. If I scroll down here, you'll see I have an update statistics command, and all I'm going to do is call this command and say update statistics for Sales.OrdersCopy. So update the stats on the indexes in that table, and I'll just execute that and it goes off. This one went relatively quickly. Depending on the size of your data and the size of your indexes, this can take a little bit of time. And now if I come back up here and run my DBCC SHOW_STATISTICS command, you will see it now knows how many rows are in the table. So it has gone through and it is figured out again how many rows are in the table. Okay. The other piece of information you can get from statistics, these other two little bits of information that I left commented out, density vector and histogram. This can be informative for you if you want to kind of see how your data is broken up. So let me just run that. The header is what's at the top. That's the same. This is the density vector, so it tells you, you know, how dense your data is. What's the average length of data? It's all date time data. So it's just dates. So average length three, which I think that's what they all are. And if you come down here, you can start to see data range rows.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-index-statistics?u=76281980&t=475)** So it shows you the key, how many rows are in that range. Okay. So this first one basically says the high key is 1/1/2013. So that defines a row. There's nothing in that range. So zero rows there. Yes. And then you can, kind of, see the average range here, one. But then you can see this guy, the high, and again, this is the high key 1/7, that has certain number of rows and how many distinct ranges. And so essentially what this is saying is between 1/2 starting on January 2nd, going up to 1/7. Here's the data about that range. And I have four distinct range rows. So I basically within that range have four distinct dates. So I probably have a second, third, fourth, fifth and I guess I don't have a sixth or seventh or some combination thereof. So this can, kind of, start to show you a breakdown of how the data in your table literally exists. And for each of these chunks, it kind of breaks these down into these different ranges. And that's partly how the index optimizer makes decisions. It figures out if you're trying to grab a range of data, if you get too large of a range. So if I were to select an entire year's worth, you know, as it gets big enough, it starts to say, "Okay, do I need to just [[Fetch]] everything? Can I grab chunks? What can I do?" And that helps the index optimizer look at your data. And it can be informative to you as you're designing indexes. Okay. So that's pretty much all there is to it.
>
> **[9:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/use-index-statistics?u=76281980&t=570)** The statistics are updated and maintained on each of your indexes. They will sort of auto compute and auto rebuild if you let them. But occasionally you might run into issues and using DBCC SHOW_STATISTICS, you can get some great value and great information about what's going on with your indexes and your stats and your data. And if need be, you can use UPDATE STATISTICS to go ahead and update those statistics a little more frequently to keep your query analyzer running and keep your queries as efficient as possible.

### Lesson 9: Developing, Deploying, and Managing Data Recovery

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Module 5 introduction](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/module-5-introduction?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/module-5-introduction?u=76281980&t=6)** As a [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] administrator, you need to maintain high availability of your [[Databases]] and have a plan in place for data recovery. Here you learn to develop, deploy, and manage data recovery solutions. We take a close look at backup devices, how to restore databases and how to define a backup and recovery strategy. Then we look at technologies to maintain high availability of your servers, as well as the best practices for disaster recovery.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Databases]] (2), [[Microsoft SQL Server|Sql server]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** sql (1)

#### [Learning objectives](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/learning-objectives-22306486?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/learning-objectives-22306486?u=76281980&t=6)** Welcome to our lesson on developing, deploying and managing data recovery. In this lesson, we look at probably the most important topic in this course, setting up a recovery plan for your [[Databases]]. If you're only going to remember one thing from this course, this is it. You may have performance issues, too many files, security holes, and a whole host of other issues with your servers but if something crashes or gets corrupted, you don't have backups from which to recover, then the entire reason for you having servers in the first place is lost. This lesson will help you understand data recovery fundamentals, understand backup devices, understand the different backup types, and how to create and verify backups, restore database, and help you define a backup and recovery strategy. Let's get started.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Databases]] (1)

#### [Understand data recovery fundamentals](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-data-recovery-fundamentals?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-data-recovery-fundamentals?u=76281980&t=6)** [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] houses a lot of important data. So you need to have a plan in place for when and if something breaks and you lose your database or you lose your server. Now typically you're going to build servers that have redundancy and hard drives or redundancy in systems, and you're going to do your best to prevent anything from ever being lost, but eventually it will happen. And in SQL Server, the primary way that you're going to make sure you're prepared for loss is through data backups. You can have secondary servers, you can have all kinds of other things, but you need to make sure you have backups in place. You have a couple of different options when it comes to backups and we have lessons that are going to walk through all of these choices. You can do full database backups, you can do partial backups, backing up just the logs, which are smaller, you can do kind of incremental backups that get everything since the last full backup. And there's different ways you can slice this and there's different ways you can set it up, but you definitely have to have that plan in place. So with that in mind, you have to also balance the consideration of time to recover versus time to back things up. And that all has to get factored into your recovery plan. So if you are very paranoid about data loss, you might backup data every 15 minutes and that's fine. You're going to be using more storage to hold more backups. And that's again, no problem. But the time to recover, that can be a little longer if you now have hundreds of files you have to restore. If you're less paranoid about data loss,
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-data-recovery-fundamentals?u=76281980&t=101)** then you can backup, say, maybe a full backup once a day. So you're spending less time doing backups. It's quicker to restore because it's just one full backup but your recovery and your potential loss period is much longer. You could lose an entire day's worth of data. So before you start diving into setting up SQL Server backups, I want you to consider how much data you can afford to lose and how much time you want to spend recovering data in the event of a loss. So that's the plan you have to make, and then you can go ahead and dive into all the other lessons we have about setting up SQL Server backups and setting up backup maintenance.

#### [Understand different types of backups](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980&t=6)** [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] supports several different types of backups, and some of these are going to be somewhat analogous to types of backups you might be familiar with if you've done [[Windows]] or server backups. So we're going to walk through and talk about each of the types of backups you can do in SQL Server. So you have [[Databases]] and that's primarily what you're going to be backing up with a [[Microsoft SQL Server]]. And there's a couple different things you can do to back up your data. So first of all, I want to take a look at this WideWorldImporter's database, and I want to look at a very specific property and talk through this because this will affect the type of backups you can do. And we'll talk more about this specifically when we talk about creating backups as well. So you'll notice, if I go to options here, the recovery model on this database is set to simple. Okay. What that basically means, [[SQL]] databases are broken up into different files. Okay. And I can click the files tab here and they will all have at least one rows data file, which is your data in your database and they will all have a log file, could have more than one log file, but you have to have at least one of each of these things for a database to even exist. The log file is where the transactions are written until such time that they are hardened, "hardened," down to the database file. So what that means is, as I'm inserting data and committing transactions, these things are all stored in this log and eventually they are check pointed and they are stored
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980&t=103)** down into the actual data. Now, these log files are just files on the hard drive, so there's not really a performance advantage to doing this. The reason this is done is for backup and recoverability. So if you think about how this log would work, let's just say I have a brand new database that I haven't touched and my log is empty, there's some data in it, like maybe I just restored it, right? And I've done nothing to this database. So 100% of my data exists in the database. A database isn't inherently incremental. So I can't just, like when I back up a Windows system, I can say, oh, well, to do an incremental backup, backup all the files that have changed since noon. Well, inside of a SQL Server database, there's no real good way to say back up all the tables that have changed since noon or backup all the rows in a table that have changed since noon because that kind of information doesn't exist. So I can back up that whole database, and that's really my only option. Because I have this log, the way this log works, these transactions are being written and committed and stored in this log file here. And eventually, they get pushed down to the database. Now, when they get pushed down to the database, is what makes this a recoverability option, in simple recovery mode, which is this here. They are periodically check pointed and pushed down to the database and that's it. The log is then truncated lather, rinse, repeat.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980&t=196)** That does not help you with recoverability. What does help you with recoverability is if I change this from simple to full. A full recovery model basically means it's going to write data to the log and it will not checkpoint that data down to the database until you back up that log. So that log contains all the changes that have happened to your database since the last time you backed up the log. So going back to our example, I have a big database I haven't touched, and I back it up. Then everything else is no longer getting written to the database data file. It's all being written to the log. And now I have that record in that log of everything that's changed since my last backup. Then I can go ahead and back up that log. All the data is truncated from that log, checkpointed down into the database, and now the database has everything, but I have that log backup, so I know everything that changed since the last time I took a full backup. And then I can take another log back up and it truncates the log and I can take another log backup and it truncates the log. And so now I have my full backup, which contained everything in the database at the moment I took it, and then I have all these log backups which contain all the changes since the last full or the last log backup. So that way I can get incremental
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980&t=290)** backups. Okay. That gives me the everything that's changed since noon feel of backing up files. Okay. So I'm going to flip this database to full so we can see the full suite of backups that are available in SQL Server. And this is where I'll take a moment to warn you. Remember, what I said was that in simple mode, it's periodically flushed to the database file, the data file and the log is truncated. So the log on a simple database typically doesn't grow very big because it has to support a transaction. So if I have large transactions, it could get a little bit bigger. But it periodically flushes itself so you don't have to worry about it. As soon as you flip to a recovery model of full, that log will not truncate until you back it up. So logs can start to grow very, very, very big. So only go to full recovery models on databases that are in an environment where you're going to have backups. So a lot of times with development databases, you don't, because you're not that worried about it. But once you've gone to a production environment with a full recovery model, you have to have these kinds of backups in place, otherwise your logs don't get truncated. All right. So that discussion aside, I'm going to click "Okay." And now my WideWorldImporter's database is in full mode, so I should be able to show you all the backup types. So to see that, I'm just going to right click and we're going to go to tasks and we're going to select backup up. Pops this backup dialog box.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980&t=382)** And it's very simple and we have a whole lesson on setting up these backups and setting up a strategy. For now, I'm just going to talk through the types of backups so you know what's available to you. Okay. So you'll see here I'm backing up the WideWorldImpoters database. That's fine. It tells me my recovery model because again, that is germane to backup. And if I drop down this backup type, you'll see I have three different backup options. They should be fairly self-explanatory, but that's why we're here. I'm going to explain them to you anyway. Full backup means backup everything you possibly can about this database, all its data, all its users, just back up the whole thing, whether it's in the data file or in the log file, we don't care. Back it all up. That's a full backup. Transaction log is what we sort of talked about when we talked about recovery model. It's everything that's in the transaction log and then that transaction log is flushed down to the database. So full is everything. Transaction log is just the transaction log, but what is that actually backing up? It's backing up everything that's changed since the last backup. Okay. So if I take a full backup that that backed up everything, including the log. If I take a log backup that only backs up the log and then flushes it down to the database. So now when I take another transaction log backup, I will only have the changes since my last transaction log backup. So your full recovery set starts to look like you have to restore this full backup and then every transaction log backup you
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980&t=479)** took after that in order. Okay. So the T-log backup is incremental and it's everything that changed since the last backup. Okay. The differential backup is everything that's changed since my last full backup. So I can take a full backup, a bunch of transaction log backups. And then if I take a differential, that differential actually backed up everything going back to my last full. So even though I took transaction log backups in between, the differential is not after the last transaction log back, it's after the last full backup. And the mix and match of why you would use full versus differential versus T-log is sort of all in your recovery and restoration planning. So how many backups do I want to be taking versus how many do I want to restore? And we have a whole lesson on designing that strategy. So for now, I just want you to know that there are these types of backups. We'll go through all these options in the backup lesson where we actually create all these things. But I just wanted to show you the types that are available and a couple other little, little options just while we're here. Your media options is how you will overwrite the media you're writing to, and we will talk about that. It doesn't matter what kind of backup you're doing. It just matters that you want to overwrite the media. You do have some options with the transaction log backups that we'll look at when we talk about transaction log backups as well. And then you have backup options, which is basically naming the set
>
> **[9:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/understand-different-types-of-backups?u=76281980&t=573)** and doing some compression and some encryption. Okay. So in general, those are your backup choices. Now, you'll also notice that WideWorldImporters here, has files and file groups. So I have a primary file group, I have a user data file group, here they are. Here's the file group broken out. You can do file group backups as well, so I can do backups that just back up the primary group or just back up the user data group. So those are also available as backup types. Okay. So there you have it. Just a quick review of the kinds of backups you can do and the important things to remember, those three types, full backup is everything in your database, differential is everything since your last full and transaction log backup is everything since either your last full or your last log backup. Okay, so remember those concepts and then there's some other lessons where we're actually going to start creating all these jobs, creating all these backups and defining a backup strategy.

#### [Create and verify backups](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/create-and-verify-backups?u=76281980)

#### [Restore a database](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/restore-a-database?u=76281980)

#### [Define a backup and recovery strategy](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980&t=6)** Obviously, taking backups of all your [[Databases]] is important and it's something you should do fairly regularly in case disaster strikes and you have to restore. However, there's more considerations to how you design your backup strategy than just backup the databases. This lesson, really, we're going to have a brief conversation, look at a couple options in the tool as we do it. But we're going to talk about things you should consider for your backup strategy. So we know there are different kinds of backups in [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]], and I'm just going to bring up the backup dialog so we can kind of talk through some of these. You have your fulls, you have your differentials, and you have your T-logs. T-logs are available when the database is not in simple recovery mode. So when you start designing a backup strategy, you have to consider a few things. First, the size of your database is going to make a bit of a difference, right? The reason is if I have a 10 gig database or 100 gig or a terabyte database, those take different amounts of time to back up. So if I have a relatively small database, something like my master database in a SQL server, I could do a full backup of that thing once an hour and it probably wouldn't take very long and it's not going to take up a whole lot of disk space. But if I have a terabyte database, that's going to take a minute to back up. So I can't just do a full backup every hour. Okay. So the size of your database is part of your consideration. The second thing you need to consider is how much data can you afford to lose?
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980&t=100)** And you typically think about that in terms of time. So this WideWorldImporter's database, if we look at some of the the tables in here, you have things in here for purchasing, sales, your warehouse. This is a fairly transactional like a good old OLTP and online transaction processing database, meaning they're taking orders, they're stocking things in their warehouse, they're selling to customers. So imagine if you were a large company that sells things online and you're taking hundreds of orders a day. How many of those orders can you afford to lose? Now, there are two answers. The executive business answer is zero, right? You're going to say it's absolutely unacceptable. We can't lose any orders. From the tech side, though, there's a reality that has to set in. I can't back up everything all the time. It isn't feasible. So you have to make a decision as to how much data you're willing to lose. Now that might be 15 minutes, so I have to back up every 15 minutes. That might be, yeah, we could lose half an hour or we could lose an hour. And for you, it's about volume and it's about data loss. So if I have a smaller [[E-Commerce]] site where I have seven orders a day, I probably don't need to back up every 15 minutes because I'm taking an order on average once every 4 hours. So I could probably say, well, I can afford to lose 4 hours of
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980&t=195)** data because nothing really happens in that time. But if you start talking about transactions, you're like, well, okay, I don't want to lose any orders, so let's back up more frequently just in case we get one, two back to back or something, right? So don't just back up every four hours because you get orders every four hours. Maybe you still back up every 30 minutes because you never know when they're going to come in. Now, larger sites are taking hundreds of orders an hour. And to some level, you have to decide, well, is it five minutes, is it 10 minutes that I can lose and by "can," it's not like it's acceptable, you're just trying to balance what you can recover with the amount of effort it's going to take to back this stuff up. Okay. Then from there, you can design your strategy. So you have the three main options in your backup that you can work with, the full, the differential and the T-log. So you might decide that for this size database, it's not that big, I'm going to take a full every day at midnight when my system's not that busy. I'm going to take a differential every day at noon, so you'll basically get everything from midnight to noon, so you'll take your full here at midnight. You'll take your differential at noon. Now, I've got everything from midnight to noon. And in between those periods, I'm taking transaction logs every hour. So 1 a.m. , 2 a.m., 3 a.m., and then if I need to recover, I could lose an hour worth of data potentially, because my T-logs are every hour, but it'll be some number between zero and an hour,
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980&t=290)** depending on when the database crashed. And when I go to recover, I just need to recover my full, my noon differential and then all the T-logs that happened after noon. If you develop a strategy where, say, you do a full backup on Sunday and then T-logs every 15 minutes throughout the week and the database crashes on a Friday night, I have to restore a full from Sunday and whatever the math is on, T-log from every 15 minutes, so it's 24 hours times four, it's 96 T-logs per day times 5 days. We're talking almost 500 transaction log backups that have to be restored. So you're balancing your recoverability with what it's going to take to restore. So you want to make it easier on yourself. So if you're going to do something like that, I would recommend maybe a full on Sunday and at least two differentials every night so that you have that sort of cushion. So now when you go to restore, it's a full differential and maybe 9DT logs. It's not a full and 500 T-logs. And also storage space. So we talked about database size. If it's a huge database like a terabyte, I'm probably not backing that up every day, I'm probably doing something more like we talked about a backup on the weekend and maybe differentials throughout the week, but you also have to consider the space to store these backups so you make these things and there's some compression and you have the option, remember, in your backup
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980&t=382)** options here, right there, to compress the data. So you're getting some compression but typically, you don't just store the one full backup, you have more than one. So you have to consider how big these things are and where you're going to put them. And so factor that into your strategy as well. And remember, if you have a full backup and I decide that every week, I'm going to overwrite that full backup with a new full backup, well, that's great, unless it's your only full backup because stuff happens all the time. So if my Sunday full backup is currently being written and I overwrote last Sunday's with the one I'm writing right now and the database decides to crash in the middle of me backing it up, you've just lost your backup. Okay. So that's another piece you have to consider, is there storage, how often do you purge that history of your database backups? And at very least, if you're taking a full backup, you should be making a new full backup that is not overwriting your old one so that you can have that backup still in place in case your new backup fails. So those are the major considerations. And then how you design your strategy is really up to you based on these options of full, differential, and T-log. I will throw one other thing at you and you have to consider your system databases. So don't forget to back those up. And those system databases are right here. And there's times that you can back them up because you know something's changed or you can back them up once a day or whatever. And let me just kind of tell you when things change in each one so you can kind of factor that in. Master's relatively small, master
>
> **[7:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980&t=479)** knows about all your server settings, it knows about all your logins, and it knows about all your databases. So unless you changed something on the server, changed the configuration, added a user or added a database, not much is going on in master, so it being backed up once a day or once a week is probably okay depending on the amount of changes that go on in your server. Your model database just serves as a template for new databases. So unless you've changed model, there's no need to back it up. So a lot of times I've been in organizations where you'll have a job set up, a [[SQL]] agent job, to backup model, and if you do change model, you kick off that job to make a backup just so you have it. You don't need to back it up every day because likely what you're going to have is 365 backups at the end of the year that are all the same. Okay. So only backup model, A, if you use it like if you rely on its settings and B, if you've changed it. MSDB, that sort of stores history. So it stores your backup histories, it stores your jobs, it stores notifications, so it stores everything kind of around this SQL Agent service down here. So jobs, alerts, proxies, operators, all this stuff, logs, error logs stored in there. Okay. So this stuff, you'll want to back up MSDB if you want to retain that history. So it's not the end of the world if you lose it, you'll lose all your jobs and you'll lose all your job history but a lot of that can be recreated. So with MSDB, again, it can grow because it
>
> **[9:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/define-a-backup-and-recovery-strategy?u=76281980&t=574)** has a lot of logging. So it can get pretty big. So back it up at least as often as you change your jobs and your operators and your proxies. If you don't necessarily care about your job history, you can back it up less frequently, your jobs will still exist. You'll just lose some history if you happen to lose that database. So factor those in as well when you're building your backup and recovery strategy. So just remember the big points here when you're starting to talk about backups is how much data can you afford to lose? That drives sort of how frequently you have to do, say, transaction log backups, how big your databases are, because the larger ones, you're not going to be able to do full backups all the time. And that's where the file and file group backups can help you too, right? So you can have a big database, but maybe it's got five file groups and I back up one on Monday and one on Tuesday, and so I kind of have this mixed state of like, yes, I have a full backup, I have each file group, but they're spread apart. And maybe that's okay because maybe some of those file groups don't change or maybe one file group, you're willing to risk more data loss than another so you can break it down to that level. So figure out how much you're willing to lose, how big your databases are. Don't forget to factor in where you're going to store all this stuff, and you should be able to come up with a backup plan that not only meets your needs, but also can make that executive happy who says you can't lose an ounce of data.

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/sql-server-2022-administration/learning-objectives-22310441?u=76281980&t=6)** Welcome to our lesson on Implementing [[Microsoft SQL Server|SQL Server]] High Availability and Disaster Recovery. If the unthinkable happens and you run into a major disaster, or even if you have a simple power outage, how do you keep your data available to your users? Having backups are important, but they take time to recover and downtime could mean lost revenue. This is where high availability solutions come into play. They help keep your data available even if you lose a system or an entire data center. This lesson covers a review of high availability and disaster recovery technologies, how to set up SQL Server log shipping and using replication for high availability. We'll also look at how to read secondary database copies. Let's get started.

And so typically, large corporations will set up high availability servers where they'll have two servers at different locations that can fail over. So we'll look at how to use that for high availability.
